   <div class="marriage-line-margin2px">&#8203;</div>&#32;<div style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:1px;">&#8203;</div>&#40;<abbr title="married">m.</abbr>&#160;2005&#59;&#32;<abbr title="divorced">div.</abbr>&#160;2010&#41;<wbr />&#8203;</div></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label">Children</th><td class="infobox-data">2</td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label">Parent</th><td class="infobox-data"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1126788409">.mw-parser-output .plainlist ol,.mw-parser-output .plainlist ul{line-height:inherit;list-style:none;margin:0;padding:0}.mw-parser-output .plainlist ol li,.mw-parser-output .plainlist ul li{margin-bottom:0}</style><div class="plainlist"><ul><li><a href="/wiki/Olu_Dara" title="Olu Dara">Olu Dara</a> (father)</li></ul></div></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label">Relatives</th><td class="infobox-data"><a href="/wiki/Bravehearts" title="Bravehearts">Jungle</a> (brother)<br /><a href="/wiki/Yara_Shahidi" title="Yara Shahidi">Yara Shahidi</a> (cousin)<br /><a href="/wiki/Sayeed_Shahidi" title="Sayeed Shahidi">Sayeed Shahidi</a> (cousin)</td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label">Awards</th><td class="infobox-data"><a href="#Awards_and_nominations">Full list</a></td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" class="infobox-full-data"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1066479718"><b>Musical career</b></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label">Genres</th><td class="infobox-data"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1129693374"><div class="hlist">
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Hip_hop_music" title="Hip hop music">Hip hop</a></li>

<li><a href="/wiki/Jazz_rap" title="Jazz rap">jazz rap</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Conscious_rap" class="mw-redirect" title="Conscious rap">conscious rap</a></li></ul>
</div></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label">Labels</th><td class="infobox-data"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1129693374"><div class="hlist">
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Mass_Appeal_Records" title="Mass Appeal Records">Mass Appeal</a></li>

<li><a href="/wiki/Ill_Will_Records" title="Ill Will Records">Ill Will</a></li>

<li><a href="/wiki/The_Orchard_(company)" title="The Orchard (company)">The Orchard</a></li></ul>
</div></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label">Formerly of</th><td class="infobox-data"><a href="/wiki/The_Firm_(hip_hop_group)" title="The Firm (hip hop group)">The Firm</a></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label">Website</th><td class="infobox-data"><span class="url"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://nasirjones.com">nasirjones<wbr />.com</a></span>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1066479718"></td></tr><tr><td colspan="2">
<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Musical artist</div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Nasir bin Olu Dara Jones</b> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="&#39;n&#39; in &#39;nigh&#39;">n</span><span title="/ɑː/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;father&#39;">ɑː</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="&#39;s&#39; in &#39;sigh&#39;">s</span><span title="/ɪər/: &#39;ear&#39; in &#39;near&#39;">ɪər</span></span>/</a></span></span>; born September 14, 1973), better known by his <a href="/wiki/Stage_name" title="Stage name">stage name</a> <b>Nas</b> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="&#39;n&#39; in &#39;nigh&#39;">n</span><span title="/ɑː/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;father&#39;">ɑː</span><span title="&#39;z&#39; in &#39;zoom&#39;">z</span></span>/</a></span></span>), is an American rapper. Rooted in <a href="/wiki/East_Coast_hip_hop" title="East Coast hip hop">East Coast hip hop</a>, he is regarded as one of the greatest rappers of all time.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> The son of jazz musician <a href="/wiki/Olu_Dara" title="Olu Dara">Olu Dara</a>, Jones began his musical career in 1989 as he adopted the moniker of "Nasty Nas" and recorded demos for <a href="/wiki/Large_Professor" title="Large Professor">Large Professor</a>. He was later featured on the 1991 song "Live at the Barbeque" by <a href="/wiki/Main_Source" title="Main Source">Main Source</a>.
</p><p>Nas's debut album, <i><a href="/wiki/Illmatic" title="Illmatic">Illmatic</a></i> (1994), received universal acclaim upon release, and is considered to be one of the greatest hip hop albums of all time;<sup id="cite_ref-about_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-about-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-prefix_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-prefix-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> in 2020, the album was inducted into the <a href="/wiki/Library_of_Congress" title="Library of Congress">Library of Congress</a>'s <a href="/wiki/National_Recording_Registry" title="National Recording Registry">National Recording Registry</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> His second album <i><a href="/wiki/It_Was_Written" title="It Was Written">It Was Written</a></i> (1996) debuted atop the <a href="/wiki/Billboard_200" title="Billboard 200"><i>Billboard</i> 200</a> and charted for four consecutive weeks; the album, along with its single "<a href="/wiki/If_I_Ruled_the_World_(Imagine_That)" title="If I Ruled the World (Imagine That)">If I Ruled the World (Imagine That)</a>" (featuring <a href="/wiki/Lauryn_Hill" title="Lauryn Hill">Lauryn Hill</a>), catapulted Nas into international success.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Both released in 1999, Nas's albums <i><a href="/wiki/I_Am..._(Nas_album)" title="I Am... (Nas album)">I Am</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Nastradamus" title="Nastradamus">Nastradamus</a></i> were criticized as inconsistent and too commercially oriented, and critics and fans feared that his output was declining in quality.
</p><p>From 2001 to 2005, Nas was involved in a highly publicized feud with <a href="/wiki/Jay-Z" title="Jay-Z">Jay-Z</a>, popularized by the <a href="/wiki/Diss_track" class="mw-redirect" title="Diss track">diss track</a> "<a href="/wiki/Ether_(song)" title="Ether (song)">Ether</a>". It was this feud, along with Nas's albums <i><a href="/wiki/Stillmatic" title="Stillmatic">Stillmatic</a></i> (2001), <i><a href="/wiki/God%27s_Son_(album)" title="God&#39;s Son (album)">God's Son</a></i> (2002), and the double album <i><a href="/wiki/Street%27s_Disciple" title="Street&#39;s Disciple">Street's Disciple</a></i> (2004), that helped restore his critical standing. After squashing the feud, Nas signed to Jay-Z's <a href="/wiki/Def_Jam_Recordings" title="Def Jam Recordings">Def Jam Recordings</a> in 2006 and went in a more provocative, politicized direction with the albums <i><a href="/wiki/Hip_Hop_Is_Dead" title="Hip Hop Is Dead">Hip Hop Is Dead</a></i> (2006) and his <a href="/wiki/Untitled_Nas_album" title="Untitled Nas album">untitled 9th studio album</a> (2008). In 2010, Nas released <i><a href="/wiki/Distant_Relatives" title="Distant Relatives">Distant Relatives</a></i>, a collaboration album with <a href="/wiki/Damian_Marley" title="Damian Marley">Damian Marley</a>, donating all royalties to charities active in Africa. His 10th studio album, <i><a href="/wiki/Life_Is_Good_(Nas_album)" title="Life Is Good (Nas album)">Life Is Good</a></i> (2012), was nominated for <a href="/wiki/Grammy_Award_for_Best_Rap_Album" title="Grammy Award for Best Rap Album">Best Rap Album</a> at the <a href="/wiki/55th_Annual_Grammy_Awards" title="55th Annual Grammy Awards">55th Annual Grammy Awards</a>. After receiving thirteen nominations, his 12th studio album, <i><a href="/wiki/King%27s_Disease" title="King&#39;s Disease">King's Disease</a></i> (2020), won him his first Grammy for Best Rap Album at the <a href="/wiki/63rd_Annual_Grammy_Awards" title="63rd Annual Grammy Awards">63rd Annual Grammy Awards</a>; he then followed it by releasing his 13th studio album, <i><a href="/wiki/King%27s_Disease_II" title="King&#39;s Disease II">King's Disease II</a></i> (2021), as the album's sequel.  In the same year, his 14th studio album, <i><a href="/wiki/Magic_(Nas_album)" title="Magic (Nas album)">Magic</a></i>, was released on Christmas Eve. <i><a href="/wiki/King%27s_Disease_III" title="King&#39;s Disease III">King's Disease III</a></i> (2022) is his fourth consecutive album with producer <a href="/wiki/Hit-Boy" title="Hit-Boy">Hit-Boy</a>.
</p><p>In 2012, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Source_(magazine)" class="mw-redirect" title="The Source (magazine)">The Source</a></i> ranked him second on their list of the "Top 50 Lyricists of All Time". In 2013, Nas was ranked 4th on MTV's "Hottest MCs in the Game" list. <a href="/wiki/About.com" class="mw-redirect" title="About.com">About.com</a> ranked him first on their list of the "50 Greatest MCs of All Time" in 2014, and a year later, Nas was featured on the "10 Best Rappers of All Time" list by <i>Billboard</i>. He is also an entrepreneur through his own record label; he serves as <a href="/wiki/Publishing" title="Publishing">associate publisher</a> of <a href="/wiki/Mass_Appeal_(media)" title="Mass Appeal (media)"><i>Mass Appeal</i></a> magazine and the co-founder of <a href="/wiki/Mass_Appeal_Records" title="Mass Appeal Records">Mass Appeal Records</a>.
Nas has released seventeen studio albums since 1994, ten of which are certified <a href="/wiki/Music_recording_certification" title="Music recording certification">gold</a>, platinum or multi-platinum in the U.S.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life">Early life</span></h2>
<p>Nasir bin Olu Dara Jones<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> was born in the <a href="/wiki/Brooklyn" title="Brooklyn">Brooklyn</a> borough of <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a> on September 14, 1973, to African American parents.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-allmusic_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-allmusic-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> His father, <a href="/wiki/Olu_Dara" title="Olu Dara">Olu Dara</a> (born Charles Jones III), is a jazz and blues musician from <a href="/wiki/Mississippi" title="Mississippi">Mississippi</a>. His mother, Fannie Ann (née Little; 1941–2002) was a <a href="/wiki/United_States_Postal_Service" title="United States Postal Service">U.S. Postal Service</a> worker from <a href="/wiki/North_Carolina" title="North Carolina">North Carolina</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> He has a brother, Jabari Fret, who raps under the name Jungle and is a member of <a href="/wiki/Hip_hop_music" title="Hip hop music">hip hop</a> group <a href="/wiki/Bravehearts" title="Bravehearts">Bravehearts</a>. His father adopted the name "Olu Dara" from the <a href="/wiki/Yoruba_people" title="Yoruba people">Yoruba people</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-findbookref1_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-findbookref1-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> "<a href="/wiki/Nasir" title="Nasir">Nasir</a>" is an Arabic name meaning "helper and protector", while "bin" means "son of" in Arabic.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> He is a cousin of actors <a href="/wiki/Yara_Shahidi" title="Yara Shahidi">Yara Shahidi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sayeed_Shahidi" title="Sayeed Shahidi">Sayeed Shahidi</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As a young child, Nas and his family relocated to the <a href="/wiki/Queensbridge_Houses" title="Queensbridge Houses">Queensbridge Houses</a> in the borough of <a href="/wiki/Queens" title="Queens">Queens</a>. His neighbor, Willy "Ill Will" Graham, influenced his interest in hip hop by playing him records.<sup id="cite_ref-Exclaim_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Exclaim-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> His parents divorced in 1985,<sup id="cite_ref-Exclaim_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Exclaim-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> and he dropped out of school after the eighth grade.<sup id="cite_ref-allmusic_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-allmusic-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> He educated himself about <a href="/wiki/African_culture" class="mw-redirect" title="African culture">African culture</a> through the <a href="/wiki/Five-Percent_Nation" title="Five-Percent Nation">Five-Percent Nation</a> (a splinter group of the <a href="/wiki/Nation_of_Islam" title="Nation of Islam">Nation of Islam</a>)<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Nuwaubian_Nation" title="Nuwaubian Nation">Nuwaubian Nation</a>. In his early years, he played the trumpet and began writing his own rhymes.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Career">Career</span></h2>
<p>As a teenager, Nas enlisted his best friend and upstairs neighbor Willie "Ill Will" Graham as his DJ. Nas initially went by the nickname <b>"Kid Wave"</b> before adopting his more commonly known alias of "Nasty Nas".<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> In 1989, then-16-year-old Nas met up with producer <a href="/wiki/Large_Professor" title="Large Professor">Large Professor</a><sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> and went to the studio where <a href="/wiki/Rakim" title="Rakim">Rakim</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kool_G_Rap" title="Kool G Rap">Kool G Rap</a> were recording their albums. When they were not in the recording studio, Nas would go into the booth and record his own material. However, none of it was ever released.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="1991.E2.80.931994:_The_beginnings_and_Illmatic"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1991–1994:_The_beginnings_and_Illmatic">1991–1994: The beginnings and <i>Illmatic</i></span></h3>
<p>In 1991, Nas performed on <a href="/wiki/Main_Source" title="Main Source">Main Source</a>'s "<a href="/wiki/Live_at_the_Barbeque" class="mw-redirect" title="Live at the Barbeque">Live at the Barbeque</a>", also produced by Large Professor. In mid-1992, Nas was approached by <a href="/wiki/MC_Serch" title="MC Serch">MC Serch</a> of <a href="/wiki/3rd_Bass" title="3rd Bass">3rd Bass</a>, who became his manager and secured Nas a record deal with <a href="/wiki/Columbia_Records" title="Columbia Records">Columbia Records</a> during the same year. Nas made his solo debut under the name of "Nasty Nas" on the single "<a href="/wiki/Halftime_(song)" title="Halftime (song)">Halftime</a>" from MC Serch's soundtrack for the film <i><a href="/wiki/Zebrahead_(film)" title="Zebrahead (film)">Zebrahead</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-allmusic_11-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-allmusic-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Called the new <a href="/wiki/Rakim" title="Rakim">Rakim</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Greatest_MCs_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greatest_MCs-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> his rhyming skills attracted a significant amount of attention within the hip hop community.
</p><p>In 1994, Nas's debut album, <i><a href="/wiki/Illmatic" title="Illmatic">Illmatic</a></i>, was released. It featured production from <a href="/wiki/Large_Professor" title="Large Professor">Large Professor</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pete_Rock" title="Pete Rock">Pete Rock</a>, <a href="/wiki/Q-Tip_(musician)" title="Q-Tip (musician)">Q-Tip</a>, <a href="/wiki/L.E.S._(producer)" class="mw-redirect" title="L.E.S. (producer)">LES</a> and <a href="/wiki/DJ_Premier" title="DJ Premier">DJ Premier</a>, as well as guest appearances from Nas's friend <a href="/wiki/AZ_(rapper)" title="AZ (rapper)">AZ</a> and his father Olu Dara. The album spawned several singles, including "The World Is Yours", "It Ain't Hard to Tell", and "One Love". Shaheem Reid of <a href="/wiki/MTV_News" title="MTV News">MTV News</a> called <i>Illmatic</i> "the first classic <a href="/wiki/Gramophone_record" class="mw-redirect" title="Gramophone record">LP</a>" of 1994.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> In 1994, Nas also recorded the song "One on One" for the soundtrack to the film <i><a href="/wiki/Street_Fighter_(1994_film)" title="Street Fighter (1994 film)">Street Fighter</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> In his book <i>To the Break of Dawn: A Freestyle on the Hip Hop Aesthetic</i>, <a href="/wiki/William_Jelani_Cobb" class="mw-redirect" title="William Jelani Cobb">William Jelani Cobb</a> writes of Nas's impact at the time:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Nas, the poetic sage of the Queensbridge projects, was hailed as the second coming of <a href="/wiki/Rakim" title="Rakim">Rakim</a>—as if the first had reached his expiration date. [...] Nas never became 'the next Rakim,' nor did he really have to. <i>Illmatic</i> stood on its own terms. The sublime lyricism of the CD, combined with the fact that it was delivered into the crucible of the boiling <a href="/wiki/East_Coast-West_Coast_hip_hop_rivalry" class="mw-redirect" title="East Coast-West Coast hip hop rivalry">East-West</a> conflict, quickly solidified [his] reputation as the premier writer of his time.<sup id="cite_ref-Cobb_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cobb-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p><i>Illmatic</i> was awarded best album of 1994 by <i><a href="/wiki/The_Source_(magazine)" class="mw-redirect" title="The Source (magazine)">The Source</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Weinstein_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weinstein-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> Steve Huey of <a href="/wiki/AllMusic" title="AllMusic">AllMusic</a> described Nas's lyrics on <i>Illmatic</i> as "highly literate" and his raps "superbly fluid regardless of the size of his vocabulary", adding that Nas is "able to evoke the bleak reality of ghetto life without losing hope or forgetting the good times".<sup id="cite_ref-allmusic_Illmatic_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-allmusic_Illmatic-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> About.com ranked <i>Illmatic</i> as the greatest hip hop album of all time,<sup id="cite_ref-about_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-about-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> and <i>Prefix</i> magazine praised it as "the best hip hop record ever made".<sup id="cite_ref-prefix_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-prefix-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="1994.E2.80.931998:_Transition_to_mainstream_direction_and_the_Firm"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1994–1998:_Transition_to_mainstream_direction_and_the_Firm">1994–1998: Transition to mainstream direction and the Firm</span></h3>
<p>In 1995, Nas did guest performances on the albums <i><a href="/wiki/Doe_or_Die" title="Doe or Die">Doe or Die</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/AZ_(rapper)" title="AZ (rapper)">AZ</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Infamous" title="The Infamous">The Infamous</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Mobb_Deep" title="Mobb Deep">Mobb Deep</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Only_Built_4_Cuban_Linx" class="mw-redirect" title="Only Built 4 Cuban Linx">Only Built 4 Cuban Linx</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Raekwon" title="Raekwon">Raekwon</a> and <i><a href="/wiki/4,5,6" title="4,5,6">4,5,6</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Kool_G_Rap" title="Kool G Rap">Kool G Rap</a>. Nas also parted ways with manager <a href="/wiki/MC_Serch" title="MC Serch">MC Serch</a>, enlisted <a href="/wiki/Steve_Stoute" title="Steve Stoute">Steve Stoute</a>, and began preparation for his second album, <i><a href="/wiki/It_Was_Written" title="It Was Written">It Was Written</a></i>. The album was chiefly produced by Tone and Poke of the <a href="/wiki/Trackmasters" title="Trackmasters">Trackmasters</a>, as Nas consciously worked towards a crossover-oriented sound. <a href="/wiki/Columbia_Records" title="Columbia Records">Columbia Records</a> had begun to pressure Nas to work towards more commercial topics, such as that of <a href="/wiki/The_Notorious_B.I.G." title="The Notorious B.I.G.">The Notorious B.I.G.</a> and had become successful by releasing street singles that still retained radio-friendly appeal. The album also expanded on Nas's Escobar persona, who lived a <i><a href="/wiki/Scarface_(1983_film)" title="Scarface (1983 film)">Scarface</a></i>/<i><a href="/wiki/Casino_(film)" class="mw-redirect" title="Casino (film)">Casino</a></i>-esque lifestyle. On the other hand, references to <i>Scarface</i> protagonist <a href="/wiki/Tony_Montana" title="Tony Montana">Tony Montana</a> notwithstanding, <i>Illmatic</i> was more about his early life growing up in the projects.<sup id="cite_ref-allmusic_11-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-allmusic-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><i>It Was Written</i> was released in mid-1996. Two singles, "<a href="/wiki/If_I_Ruled_the_World_(Imagine_That)" title="If I Ruled the World (Imagine That)">If I Ruled the World (Imagine That)</a>" (featuring <a href="/wiki/Lauryn_Hill" title="Lauryn Hill">Lauryn Hill</a> of <a href="/wiki/The_Fugees" class="mw-redirect" title="The Fugees">The Fugees</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Street_Dreams_(song)" title="Street Dreams (song)">"Street Dreams"</a> (including a remix with <a href="/wiki/R._Kelly" title="R. Kelly">R. Kelly</a>), were instant hits.<sup id="cite_ref-billboard_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-billboard-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> These songs were promoted by big-budget music videos directed by <a href="/wiki/Hype_Williams" title="Hype Williams">Hype Williams</a>, making Nas a common name among mainstream <a href="/wiki/Hip_hop_music" title="Hip hop music">hip-hop</a>. Reviewing <i>It Was Written</i>, Leo Stanley of Allmusic believed the album's rhymes were not as complex as those of <i>Illmatic</i>, but still thought Nas had "deepened his talents, creating a complex series of rhymes that not only flow, but manage to tell coherent stories as well."<sup id="cite_ref-allmusic_It_Was_Written_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-allmusic_It_Was_Written-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> <i>It Was Written</i> featured the debut of <a href="/wiki/The_Firm_(hip_hop_group)" title="The Firm (hip hop group)">the Firm</a>, a supergroup consisting of Nas, <a href="/wiki/AZ_(rapper)" title="AZ (rapper)">AZ</a>, <a href="/wiki/Foxy_Brown_(rapper)" title="Foxy Brown (rapper)">Foxy Brown</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cormega" title="Cormega">Cormega</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>Signed to <a href="/wiki/Dr._Dre" title="Dr. Dre">Dr. Dre</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Aftermath_Entertainment" title="Aftermath Entertainment">Aftermath Entertainment</a> label, the Firm began working on their debut album. Halfway through the production of the album, <a href="/wiki/Cormega" title="Cormega">Cormega</a> was fired from the group by <a href="/wiki/Steve_Stoute" title="Steve Stoute">Steve Stoute</a>, who had unsuccessfully attempted to force Cormega to sign a deal with his management company. Cormega subsequently became one of Nas's most vocal opponents and released a number of <a href="/wiki/Underground_hip_hop" title="Underground hip hop">underground hip hop</a> singles <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/diss" class="extiw" title="wikt:diss">dissing</a> Nas, Stoute, and <a href="/wiki/Nature_(rapper)" title="Nature (rapper)">Nature</a>, who replaced Cormega as the fourth member of the Firm.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/The_Album_(The_Firm_album)" title="The Album (The Firm album)">Nas, Foxy Brown, AZ, and Nature Present The Firm: The Album</a></i> was finally released in 1997 to mixed reviews. The album failed to live up to its expected sales despite being certified platinum, and the members of the group disbanded to go their separate ways.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>During this period, Nas was one of four rappers (the others being <a href="/wiki/B-Real" title="B-Real">B-Real</a>, <a href="/wiki/KRS-One" title="KRS-One">KRS-One</a> and <a href="/wiki/RBX" title="RBX">RBX</a>) in the hip-hop supergroup <a href="/wiki/Group_Therapy_(group)" class="mw-redirect" title="Group Therapy (group)">Group Therapy</a>, who appeared on the song "<a href="/wiki/East_Coast/West_Coast_Killas" class="mw-redirect" title="East Coast/West Coast Killas">East Coast/West Coast Killas</a>" from <i><a href="/wiki/Dr._Dre_Presents_the_Aftermath" class="mw-redirect" title="Dr. Dre Presents the Aftermath">Dr. Dre Presents the Aftermath</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="1998.E2.80.932001:_Heightened_commercial_direction_and_inconsistent_output"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1998–2001:_Heightened_commercial_direction_and_inconsistent_output">1998–2001: Heightened commercial direction and inconsistent output</span></h3>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Nas-04.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Nas-04.jpg/220px-Nas-04.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="222" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Nas-04.jpg/330px-Nas-04.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Nas-04.jpg/440px-Nas-04.jpg 2x" data-file-width="948" data-file-height="958" /></a><figcaption>Nas in 1998</figcaption></figure>
<p>In late 1998, Nas began working on a double album, to be entitled <i>I Am... The Autobiography</i>; he intended it as the middle ground between <i>Illmatic</i> and <i>It Was Written</i>, with each track detailing a part of his life.<sup id="cite_ref-allmusic_11-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-allmusic-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> In 1998, Nas co-wrote and starred in <a href="/wiki/Hype_Williams" title="Hype Williams">Hype Williams</a>'s feature film <i><a href="/wiki/Belly_(film)" title="Belly (film)">Belly</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-allmusic_11-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-allmusic-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> <i>I Am... The Autobiography</i> was completed in early 1999, and a music video was shot for its lead single, "<a href="/wiki/Nas_Is_Like" title="Nas Is Like">Nas Is Like</a>". It was produced by <a href="/wiki/DJ_Premier" title="DJ Premier">DJ Premier</a> and contained vocal samples from "<a href="/wiki/It_Ain%27t_Hard_to_Tell" title="It Ain&#39;t Hard to Tell">It Ain't Hard to Tell</a>". Music critic M.F. DiBella noticed that Nas also covered "politics, the state of hip-hop, <a href="/wiki/Y2K" class="mw-redirect" title="Y2K">Y2K</a>, <a href="/wiki/Race_(classification_of_human_beings)" class="mw-redirect" title="Race (classification of human beings)">race</a>, and religion with his own unique perspective" in the album besides autobiographical lyrics.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> Much of the LP was leaked into MP3 format onto the Internet, and Nas and Stoute quickly recorded enough substitute material to constitute a single-disc release.<sup id="cite_ref-Weinstein_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weinstein-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The second single on <i><a href="/wiki/I_Am..._(Nas_album)" title="I Am... (Nas album)">I Am...</a></i> was "<a href="/wiki/Hate_Me_Now" title="Hate Me Now">Hate Me Now</a>", featuring <a href="/wiki/Sean_Combs" title="Sean Combs">Sean "Puffy" Combs</a>, which was used as an example by Nas's critics accusing him of moving towards more commercial themes. The video featured Nas and Combs being <a href="/wiki/Crucifixion" title="Crucifixion">crucified</a> in a manner similar to Jesus Christ; after the video was completed, Combs requested his crucifixion scene be edited out of the video. However, the unedited copy of the "Hate Me Now" video made its way to MTV. Within minutes of the broadcast, Combs and his bodyguards allegedly made their way into <a href="/wiki/Steve_Stoute" title="Steve Stoute">Steve Stoute</a>'s office and assaulted him, at one point apparently hitting Stoute over the head with a champagne bottle. Stoute pressed charges, but he and Combs <a href="/wiki/Legal_settlement" class="mw-redirect" title="Legal settlement">settled out-of-court</a> that June.<sup id="cite_ref-Weinstein_29-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weinstein-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> Columbia had scheduled to release the <a href="/wiki/Music_piracy" title="Music piracy">infringed</a> material from <i>I Am...</i> under the title <i><a href="/wiki/Nastradamus" title="Nastradamus">Nastradamus</a></i> during the later half of 1999, but, at the last minute, Nas decided to record an entire new album for the 1999 release of <i><a href="/wiki/Nastradamus" title="Nastradamus">Nastradamus</a></i>. <i>Nastradamus</i> was therefore rushed to meet a November release date. Though critical reviews were unfavorable, it did result in a minor hit, "<a href="/wiki/You_Owe_Me_(Nas_song)" title="You Owe Me (Nas song)">You Owe Me</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-allmusic_11-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-allmusic-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Fans and critics feared that Nas's career was declining, artistically and commercially, as both <i>I Am...</i> and <i>Nastradamus</i> were criticized as inconsistent and overtly-commercialized.<sup id="cite_ref-cowie_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cowie-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2000, <i><a href="/wiki/Nas_%26_Ill_Will_Records_Presents_QB%27s_Finest" title="Nas &amp; Ill Will Records Presents QB&#39;s Finest">Nas &amp; Ill Will Records Presents QB's Finest</a></i>, which is popularly known as simply <i>QB's Finest</i>, was released on Nas's <a href="/wiki/Ill_Will_Records" title="Ill Will Records">Ill Will Records</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-allmusic_11-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-allmusic-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> <i>QB's Finest</i> is a compilation album that featured Nas and a number of other rappers from <a href="/wiki/Queensbridge_Houses" title="Queensbridge Houses">Queensbridge</a> projects, including <a href="/wiki/Mobb_Deep" title="Mobb Deep">Mobb Deep</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nature_(rapper)" title="Nature (rapper)">Nature</a>, <a href="/wiki/Capone-N-Noreaga" title="Capone-N-Noreaga">Capone</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Bravehearts" title="Bravehearts">Bravehearts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tragedy_Khadafi" title="Tragedy Khadafi">Tragedy Khadafi</a>, Millennium Thug and <a href="/wiki/Cormega" title="Cormega">Cormega</a>, who had briefly reconciled with Nas. The album also featured guest appearances from Queensbridge hip-hop legends <a href="/wiki/Roxanne_Shant%C3%A9" class="mw-redirect" title="Roxanne Shanté">Roxanne Shanté</a>, <a href="/wiki/MC_Shan" title="MC Shan">MC Shan</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Marley_Marl" title="Marley Marl">Marley Marl</a>. Shan and Marley Marl both appeared on the lead single "Da Bridge 2001", which was based on Shan &amp; Marl's 1986 recording "The Bridge".<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="2001.E2.80.932006:_Feud_with_Jay-Z.2C_Stillmatic.2C_God.27s_Son.2C_and_double_album"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="2001–2006:_Feud_with_Jay-Z,_Stillmatic,_God's_Son,_and_double_album">2001–2006: Feud with Jay-Z, <i>Stillmatic</i>, <i>God's Son</i>, and double album</span></h3>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-left" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Nas-01-mika.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Nas-01-mika.jpg/220px-Nas-01-mika.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="222" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Nas-01-mika.jpg/330px-Nas-01-mika.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Nas-01-mika.jpg/440px-Nas-01-mika.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1122" data-file-height="1134" /></a><figcaption>Nas performing in 2003</figcaption></figure>
<p>After trading veiled criticisms on various songs, freestyles and mixtape appearances, the highly publicised dispute between Nas and <a href="/wiki/Jay-Z" title="Jay-Z">Jay-Z</a> became widely known to the public in 2001.<sup id="cite_ref-allmusic_11-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-allmusic-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Jay-Z, in his song "<a href="/wiki/Takeover_(Jay-Z_song)" title="Takeover (Jay-Z song)">Takeover</a>", criticised Nas by calling him "fake" and his career "lame".<sup id="cite_ref-Return_to_the_Dis_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Return_to_the_Dis-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> Nas responded with "<a href="/wiki/Ether_(song)" title="Ether (song)">Ether</a>", in which he compared Jay-Z to such characters as <a href="/wiki/J.J._Evans" class="mw-redirect" title="J.J. Evans">J.J. Evans</a> from the sitcom <i><a href="/wiki/Good_Times" title="Good Times">Good Times</a></i> and cigarette company mascot <a href="/wiki/Joe_Camel" title="Joe Camel">Joe Camel</a>. The song was included on Nas's fifth studio album, <i><a href="/wiki/Stillmatic" title="Stillmatic">Stillmatic</a></i>, released in December 2001. His daughter, Destiny, is listed as an executive producer on <i> Stillmatic</i> so she could receive royalty checks from the album.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> <i>Stillmatic</i> peaked at No. 5 on the U.S. <a href="/wiki/Billboard_200" title="Billboard 200"><i>Billboard</i> 200</a> chart and featured the singles "<a href="/wiki/Got_Ur_Self_A..." class="mw-redirect" title="Got Ur Self A...">Got Ur Self A...</a>" and "<a href="/wiki/One_Mic" title="One Mic">One Mic</a>".
</p><p>In response to "Ether", Jay-Z released the song "Supa Ugly", which <a href="/wiki/WQHT" title="WQHT">Hot 97</a> radio host <a href="/wiki/Angie_Martinez" title="Angie Martinez">Angie Martinez</a> premiered on December 11, 2001.<sup id="cite_ref-Return_to_the_Dis_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Return_to_the_Dis-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> In the song, Jay-Z explicitly boasts about having an affair with Nas's girlfriend, Carmen Bryan.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> New York City hip-hop radio station <a href="/wiki/WQHT" title="WQHT">Hot 97</a> issued a poll asking listeners which rapper made the better <a href="/wiki/Diss_song" class="mw-redirect" title="Diss song">diss song</a>; Nas won with 58% while Jay-Z got 42% of the votes.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> In 2002, in the midst of the dispute between the two New York rappers, <a href="/wiki/Eminem" title="Eminem">Eminem</a> cited both Nas and Jay-Z as being two of the best MCs in the industry, in his song <a href="/wiki/%27Till_I_Collapse" title="&#39;Till I Collapse">'Till I Collapse</a>. Both the dispute and <i>Stillmatic</i> signaled an artistic comeback for Nas after a string of inconsistent albums.<sup id="cite_ref-Rabin_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rabin-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/The_Lost_Tapes_(Nas_album)" title="The Lost Tapes (Nas album)">The Lost Tapes</a></i>, a compilation of previously unreleased or bootlegged songs from 1998 to 2001, was released by Columbia in September 2002. The collection attained respectable sales and received rave reviews from critics.<sup id="cite_ref-Weinstein_29-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weinstein-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In December 2002, Nas released the <i><a href="/wiki/God%27s_Son_(album)" title="God&#39;s Son (album)">God's Son</a></i> album including its lead single, "Made You Look" which used a pitched down sample of the <a href="/wiki/Incredible_Bongo_Band" title="Incredible Bongo Band">Incredible Bongo Band</a>'s "<a href="/wiki/Apache_(instrumental)" title="Apache (instrumental)">Apache</a>". The album peaked at No. 12 on the <a href="/wiki/Billboard_Hot_100" title="Billboard Hot 100"><i>Billboard</i> Hot 100</a> and No. 1 on the <a href="/wiki/Top_R%26B/Hip-Hop_Albums" title="Top R&amp;B/Hip-Hop Albums">Top R&amp;B/Hip-Hop Albums</a> charts despite widespread Internet bootlegging.<sup id="cite_ref-album_charts_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-album_charts-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> Time Magazine named his album best hip-hop album of the year. <a href="/wiki/Vibe_(magazine)" title="Vibe (magazine)">Vibe</a> gave it four stars and <a href="/wiki/The_Source_(magazine)" class="mw-redirect" title="The Source (magazine)">The Source</a> gave it four mics. The second single, "<a href="/wiki/I_Can_(Nas_song)" title="I Can (Nas song)">I Can</a>", which reworked elements from <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_van_Beethoven" title="Ludwig van Beethoven">Beethoven</a>'s "<a href="/wiki/F%C3%BCr_Elise" title="Für Elise">Für Elise</a>", became Nas's biggest hit to date in 2003, garnering substantial radio airplay on urban, <a href="/wiki/Rhythmic_contemporary" title="Rhythmic contemporary">rhythmic</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Top_40" title="Top 40">top 40</a> radio stations, as well as on the MTV and <a href="/wiki/VH1" title="VH1">VH1</a> music video networks. <i>God's Son</i> also includes several songs dedicated to Nas's mother, who died of cancer in April 2002, including "Dance". In 2003, Nas was featured on the <a href="/wiki/Korn" title="Korn">Korn</a> song "Play Me", from Korn's <i><a href="/wiki/Take_a_Look_in_the_Mirror" title="Take a Look in the Mirror">Take a Look in the Mirror</a></i> LP. Also in 2003, a live performance in New York City, featuring <a href="/wiki/Ludacris" title="Ludacris">Ludacris</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jadakiss" title="Jadakiss">Jadakiss</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Darryl_McDaniels" title="Darryl McDaniels">Darryl McDaniels</a> (of <a href="/wiki/Run-D.M.C." class="mw-redirect" title="Run-D.M.C.">Run-D.M.C.</a> fame), was released on DVD as <i>Made You Look: God's Son Live</i>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p><i>God's Son</i> was critical in the power struggle between Nas and Jay-Z in the hip-hop industry at the time. In an article at the time, Joseph Jones of <a href="/wiki/PopMatters" title="PopMatters">PopMatters</a> stated, "Whether you like it or not, "Ether" did this. With <i>God's Son</i>, Nas has the opportunity to cement his status as the King of NY, at least for another 3-4-year term, or he could prove that he is not the savior that hip-hop fans should be pinning their hopes on."<sup id="cite_ref-Jones_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jones-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> After the album's release, he began helping <a href="/wiki/The_Bravehearts" class="mw-redirect" title="The Bravehearts">the Bravehearts</a>, an act including his younger brother Jungle and friend Wiz (Wizard), put together their debut album, <i>Bravehearted</i>. The album featured guest appearances from Nas, Nashawn (Millennium Thug), <a href="/wiki/Lil_Jon" title="Lil Jon">Lil Jon</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Jully_Black" title="Jully Black">Jully Black</a>.
</p><p>Nas released his seventh album <i><a href="/wiki/Street%27s_Disciple" title="Street&#39;s Disciple">Street's Disciple</a></i>, a sprawling double album, on November 30, 2004. It addressed subject matter both political and personal, including his impending marriage to recording artist <a href="/wiki/Kelis" title="Kelis">Kelis</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-allmusic_11-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-allmusic-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> The double-sided single "<a href="/wiki/Thief%27s_Theme" title="Thief&#39;s Theme">Thief's Theme</a>"/"You Know My Style" was released months before the album's release, followed by the single "<a href="/wiki/Bridging_the_Gap_(song)" title="Bridging the Gap (song)">Bridging the Gap</a>" upon the album's release. Although <i>Street's Disciple</i> went <a href="/wiki/Platinum_record" class="mw-redirect" title="Platinum record">platinum</a>, it served as a drop-off from Nas's previous commercial successes.<sup id="cite_ref-allmusic_11-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-allmusic-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2005, New York-based rapper <a href="/wiki/50_Cent" title="50 Cent">50 Cent</a> dissed Nas on his song "<a href="/wiki/Piggy_Bank_(song)" title="Piggy Bank (song)">Piggy Bank</a>", which brought his reputation into question in hip-hop circles.<sup id="cite_ref-allmusic_11-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-allmusic-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> In October, Nas made a surprise appearance at Jay-Z's "I Declare War" concert, where they reconciled their beef.<sup id="cite_ref-allmusic_11-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-allmusic-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> At the show, Jay-Z announced to the crowd, "It's bigger than 'I Declare War'. Let's go, Esco!" and Nas then joined him onstage,<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> and the two performed Jay-Z's "<a href="/wiki/Dead_Presidents_(song)" title="Dead Presidents (song)">Dead Presidents</a>" (1996) together, a song that featured a prominent sample of Nas's 1994 track, "<a href="/wiki/The_World_Is_Yours_(Nas_song)" title="The World Is Yours (Nas song)">The World Is Yours</a>" (1994).<sup id="cite_ref-allmusic_11-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-allmusic-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="2006.E2.80.932008:_Hip_Hop_Is_Dead.2C_Untitled.2C_and_politicized_efforts"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="2006–2008:_Hip_Hop_Is_Dead,_Untitled,_and_politicized_efforts">2006–2008: <i>Hip Hop Is Dead</i>, Untitled, and politicized efforts</span></h3>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Nas_in_Ottawa,_2007.JPG" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Nas_in_Ottawa%2C_2007.JPG/200px-Nas_in_Ottawa%2C_2007.JPG" decoding="async" width="200" height="237" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Nas_in_Ottawa%2C_2007.JPG/300px-Nas_in_Ottawa%2C_2007.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Nas_in_Ottawa%2C_2007.JPG/400px-Nas_in_Ottawa%2C_2007.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1839" data-file-height="2178" /></a><figcaption>Nas performing in <a href="/wiki/Ottawa" title="Ottawa">Ottawa</a>, 2007</figcaption></figure>
<p>The reconciliation between Nas and Jay-Z created the opportunity for Nas to sign a deal with <a href="/wiki/Def_Jam_Recordings" title="Def Jam Recordings">Def Jam Recordings</a>, of which Jay-Z was president at the time.<sup id="cite_ref-allmusic_11-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-allmusic-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Jay-Z signed Nas on January 23, 2006;<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> the signing included an agreement that Nas was to be paid about $3,000,000, including a recording budget, for each of his first two albums with Def Jam.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>Tentatively called <i>Hip Hop Is Dead...The N</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-Reid2006_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reid2006-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Hip_Hop_Is_Dead" title="Hip Hop Is Dead">Hip Hop Is Dead</a></i> was a commentary on the state of hip-hop and featured "Black Republican", a hyped collaboration with Jay-Z.<sup id="cite_ref-allmusic_11-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-allmusic-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> The album debuted on Def Jam and Nas new imprint at that label, The Jones Experience, at No. 1 on the <a href="/wiki/Billboard_200" title="Billboard 200"><i>Billboard</i> 200</a> charts, selling 355,000 copies—Nas's third number one album, along with <i>It Was Written</i> and <i>I Am...</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> It also inspired reactions about the state of hip-hop,<sup id="cite_ref-allmusic_11-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-allmusic-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> particularly controversy with <a href="/wiki/Southern_hip_hop" title="Southern hip hop">Southern hip hop</a> artists who felt the album's title was a criticism aimed at them.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> Nas's 2004 song, "Thief's Theme", was featured in the 2006 film, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Departed" title="The Departed">The Departed</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> Nas's former label, <a href="/wiki/Columbia_Records" title="Columbia Records">Columbia Records</a>, released the compilation <i><a href="/wiki/Greatest_Hits_(Nas_album)" title="Greatest Hits (Nas album)">Greatest Hits</a></i> in November.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On October 12, 2007, Nas announced that his next album would be called <i>Nigger</i>. Both <a href="/wiki/Progressivism" title="Progressivism">progressive</a> commentators, such as <a href="/wiki/Jesse_Jackson" title="Jesse Jackson">Jesse Jackson</a> and <a href="/wiki/Al_Sharpton" title="Al Sharpton">Al Sharpton</a>, and the conservative-aligned news channel <a href="/wiki/Fox_News" title="Fox News">Fox News</a> were outraged; Jackson called on entertainers to stop using the epithet after comedian <a href="/wiki/Michael_Richards" title="Michael Richards">Michael Richards</a> used it onstage in late 2006.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> Controversy escalated as the album's impending release date drew nearer, going as far as to spark rumors that <a href="/wiki/Def_Jam" class="mw-redirect" title="Def Jam">Def Jam</a> was planning to drop Nas unless he changed the title.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> Additionally, then-<a href="/wiki/Fort_Greene,_Brooklyn" title="Fort Greene, Brooklyn">Fort Greene, Brooklyn</a> assemblyman (later United States Representative) <a href="/wiki/Hakeem_Jeffries" title="Hakeem Jeffries">Hakeem Jeffries</a> requested that New York State Comptroller <a href="/wiki/Thomas_DiNapoli" title="Thomas DiNapoli">Thomas DiNapoli</a> withdraw $84,000,000 from the state <a href="/wiki/Pension_fund" title="Pension fund">pension fund</a> that had been invested into <a href="/wiki/Universal_Music_Group" title="Universal Music Group">Universal</a> and its parent company, <a href="/wiki/Vivendi" title="Vivendi">Vivendi</a>, if the album's title was not changed.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> On the opposite side of the spectrum, many of the most famous names in the entertainment industry supported Nas for using the racial epithet as the title of his full-length LP.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> Nas's management worried the album would not be sold by chain stores such as <a href="/wiki/Wal-Mart" class="mw-redirect" title="Wal-Mart">Wal-Mart</a>, thus limiting its distribution.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On May 19, 2008, Nas decided to forgo an album title.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> Responding to <a href="/wiki/Jesse_Jackson" title="Jesse Jackson">Jesse Jackson</a>'s remarks and use of the word "nigger", Nas called him "the biggest player hater", stating "His time is up. All you old niggas' time is up. We heard your voice, we saw your marching, we heard your sermons. We don't want to hear that shit no more. It's a new day. It's a new voice. I'm here now. We don't need Jesse; I'm here. I got this. We the voice now. It's no more Jesse. Sorry. Goodbye. You ain't helping nobody in the 'hood and that's the bottom line."<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> He also said of the album's title: "It's important to me that this album gets to the fans. It's been a long time coming. I want my fans to know that creatively and lyrically, they can expect the same content and the same messages. The people will always know what the real title of this album is and what to call it."<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The album was ultimately released on July 15, 2008, untitled. It featured production from <a href="/wiki/Polow_da_Don" title="Polow da Don">Polow da Don</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stic.man" title="Stic.man">stic.man</a> of <a href="/wiki/Dead_Prez" title="Dead Prez">Dead Prez</a>, Sons of Light and J. Myers,<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> "<a href="/wiki/Hero_(Nas_song)" title="Hero (Nas song)">Hero</a>", the album's lead single released on June 23, 2008, reached No. 97 on the <i>Billboard</i> Hot 100 and No. 87 on the Hot R&amp;B/Hip-Hop Singles &amp; Tracks.<sup id="cite_ref-single_charts_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-single_charts-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> In July, Nas attained a shoe deal with <a href="/wiki/Fila_(company)" class="mw-redirect" title="Fila (company)">Fila</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> In an interview with <a href="/wiki/MTV_News" title="MTV News">MTV News</a> in July, Nas speculated that he might release two albums: one produced by <a href="/wiki/DJ_Premier" title="DJ Premier">DJ Premier</a> and another by <a href="/wiki/Dr._Dre" title="Dr. Dre">Dr. Dre</a>—simultaneously the same day.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> Nas worked on Dr. Dre's studio album <i><a href="/wiki/Detox_(Dr._Dre_album)" class="mw-redirect" title="Detox (Dr. Dre album)">Detox</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> Nas was also awarded 'Emcee of the Year' in the HipHopDX 2008 Awards for his latest solo effort, the quality of his appearances on other albums and was described as having "become an artist who thrives off of reinvention and going against the system."<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="2009.E2.80.932012:_Distant_Relatives_and_Life_Is_Good"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="2009–2012:_Distant_Relatives_and_Life_Is_Good">2009–2012: <i>Distant Relatives</i> and <i>Life Is Good</i></span></h3>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-left" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Nas_and_Damian_Marley_performing_in_Wellington_Photo_By_Brady_Dyer.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Nas_and_Damian_Marley_performing_in_Wellington_Photo_By_Brady_Dyer.jpg/240px-Nas_and_Damian_Marley_performing_in_Wellington_Photo_By_Brady_Dyer.jpg" decoding="async" width="240" height="160" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Nas_and_Damian_Marley_performing_in_Wellington_Photo_By_Brady_Dyer.jpg/360px-Nas_and_Damian_Marley_performing_in_Wellington_Photo_By_Brady_Dyer.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Nas_and_Damian_Marley_performing_in_Wellington_Photo_By_Brady_Dyer.jpg/480px-Nas_and_Damian_Marley_performing_in_Wellington_Photo_By_Brady_Dyer.jpg 2x" data-file-width="900" data-file-height="600" /></a><figcaption>Nas and <a href="/wiki/Damian_Marley" title="Damian Marley">Damian Marley</a> performing in <a href="/wiki/Wellington" title="Wellington">Wellington</a>, 2011</figcaption></figure>
<p>At the <a href="/wiki/51st_Grammy_Awards" class="mw-redirect" title="51st Grammy Awards">2009 Grammy Awards</a>, Nas confirmed he was collaborating on an album with <a href="/wiki/Reggae" title="Reggae">reggae</a> singer <a href="/wiki/Damian_Marley" title="Damian Marley">Damian Marley</a> which was expected to be released in late 2009. Nas said of the collaboration in an interview "I was a big fan of his father and of course all the children, all the offspring, and Damian, I kind of looked at Damian as a rap guy. His stuff is not really singing, or if he does, it comes off more hard, like on some street shit. I always liked how reggae and hip-hop have always been intertwined and always kind of pushed each other, I always liked the connection. I'd worked with people before from the reggae world but when I worked with Damian, the whole workout was perfect".<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> A portion of the profit was planned to go towards building a school in Africa.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> He went on to say that it was "too early to tell the title or anything like that".<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/The_Los_Angeles_Times" class="mw-redirect" title="The Los Angeles Times">The Los Angeles Times</a></i> reported that the album would be titled <i><a href="/wiki/Distant_Relatives" title="Distant Relatives">Distant Relatives</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>
Nas also revealed that he would begin working on his tenth studio album following the release of <i>Distant Relatives</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> During late 2009, Nas used his live band Mulatto with music director Dustin Moore for concerts in Europe and Australia.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>After announcing a possible release in 2010,<sup id="cite_ref-Nas_on_The_Lost_Tapes_Vol._2_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nas_on_The_Lost_Tapes_Vol._2-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> a follow-up compilation to <i><a href="/wiki/The_Lost_Tapes_(Nas_album)" title="The Lost Tapes (Nas album)">The Lost Tapes</a></i> (2002) was delayed indefinitely due to issues between him and Def Jam.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> His eleventh studio album, <i><a href="/wiki/Life_Is_Good_(Nas_album)" title="Life Is Good (Nas album)">Life Is Good</a></i> (2012) was produced primarily by Salaam Remi and No I.D, and released on July 13, 2012. Nas called the album a "magic moment" in his rap career.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2011, Nas announced that he would release collaboration albums with <a href="/wiki/Mobb_Deep" title="Mobb Deep">Mobb Deep</a>, <a href="/wiki/Common_(rapper)" title="Common (rapper)">Common</a>, and a third with <a href="/wiki/DJ_Premier" title="DJ Premier">DJ Premier</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-hiphopdx1_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hiphopdx1-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-sohh_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sohh-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> Common said of the project in a 2011 interview, "At some point, we will do that. We'd talked about it and we had a good idea to call it <i>Nas.Com</i>. That was actually going to be a mixtape at one point. But we decided that we should make it an album."<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> <i>Life is Good</i> would be nominated for <a href="/wiki/Best_Rap_Album" class="mw-redirect" title="Best Rap Album">Best Rap Album</a> at the <a href="/wiki/2013_Grammy_Awards" class="mw-redirect" title="2013 Grammy Awards">2013 Grammy Awards</a>.
</p>
<h3><span id="2013.E2.80.932019:_Nasir_and_The_Lost_Tapes_2"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="2013–2019:_Nasir_and_The_Lost_Tapes_2">2013–2019: <i>Nasir</i> and <i>The Lost Tapes 2</i></span></h3>
<p>In January 2013, Nas announced he had begun working on his twelfth studio album, which would be his final album for Def Jam.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> The album was supposed to be released during 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-huffingtonpost1_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-huffingtonpost1-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> In October 2013, DJ Premier said that his collaboration album with Nas, would be released following his twelfth studio album.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> In October 2013, Nas confirmed that a rumored song "Sinatra in the Sands" featuring <a href="/wiki/Jay-Z" title="Jay-Z">Jay-Z</a>, <a href="/wiki/Justin_Timberlake" title="Justin Timberlake">Justin Timberlake</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Timbaland" title="Timbaland">Timbaland</a> would be featured on the album.<sup id="cite_ref-huffingtonpost1_83-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-huffingtonpost1-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On April 16, 2014, on the twentieth anniversary of <i>Illmatic</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-The_Detroit_News_2014-10-10_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Detroit_News_2014-10-10-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> the documentary <i><a href="/wiki/Nas:_Time_Is_Illmatic" title="Nas: Time Is Illmatic">Nas: Time Is Illmatic</a></i> was premiered which recounted circumstances leading up to Nas's debut album.<sup id="cite_ref-LA_Times_2014-10-01_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LA_Times_2014-10-01-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> It was reported on September 10, that Nas has finished his last album with Def Jam.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> On October 30, Nas released a song which might have been the first single on his new album, titled "The Season", produced by <a href="/wiki/J_Dilla" title="J Dilla">J Dilla</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> Nas has also collaborated with the Australian hip-hop group, <a href="/wiki/Bliss_n_Eso" title="Bliss n Eso">Bliss n Eso</a>, in 2014. They released the track "I Am Somebody" in May 2014. Nas was featured on the song "We Are" from <a href="/wiki/Justin_Bieber" title="Justin Bieber">Justin Bieber</a>'s fourth studio album, <i><a href="/wiki/Purpose_(Justin_Bieber_album)" title="Purpose (Justin Bieber album)">Purpose</a></i>, released in November 2015.
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:NAS_performing_at_the_2015_Sugar_Mountain_festival,_Melbourne,_Australia.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/NAS_performing_at_the_2015_Sugar_Mountain_festival%2C_Melbourne%2C_Australia.jpg/170px-NAS_performing_at_the_2015_Sugar_Mountain_festival%2C_Melbourne%2C_Australia.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="227" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/NAS_performing_at_the_2015_Sugar_Mountain_festival%2C_Melbourne%2C_Australia.jpg/255px-NAS_performing_at_the_2015_Sugar_Mountain_festival%2C_Melbourne%2C_Australia.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/NAS_performing_at_the_2015_Sugar_Mountain_festival%2C_Melbourne%2C_Australia.jpg/340px-NAS_performing_at_the_2015_Sugar_Mountain_festival%2C_Melbourne%2C_Australia.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1536" data-file-height="2048" /></a><figcaption>Nas performing at the 2015 Sugar Mountain festival in <a href="/wiki/Melbourne" title="Melbourne">Melbourne</a></figcaption></figure>
<p>Nas was announced as one of the executive producers of the Netflix original series, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Get_Down" title="The Get Down">The Get Down</a></i>, prior to its release in August 2016. He narrated the series and rapped as adult Ezekiel of 1996.
</p><p>On October 16, 2016, he received the Jimmy Iovine Icon Award at 2016 REVOLT Music Conference for having a lasting impact and unique influence on music, numerous years in the rap business, his partnership with Hennessy, and Mass Appeal imprint by <a href="/wiki/Puff_Daddy" class="mw-redirect" title="Puff Daddy">Puff Daddy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> In November 2016, Nas collaborated with <a href="/wiki/Lin-Manuel_Miranda" title="Lin-Manuel Miranda">Lin-Manuel Miranda</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dave_East" title="Dave East">Dave East</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aloe_Blacc" title="Aloe Blacc">Aloe Blacc</a> on a song called "Wrote My Way Out", which appears on <i><a href="/wiki/The_Hamilton_Mixtape" title="The Hamilton Mixtape">The Hamilton Mixtape</a></i>. On April 12, 2017, Nas released the song <i>Angel Dust</i> as soundtrack for TV series <i><a href="/wiki/The_Getdown" class="mw-redirect" title="The Getdown">The Getdown</a></i>. It contains a sample of the <a href="/wiki/Gil_Scott-Heron" title="Gil Scott-Heron">Gil Scott-Heron</a> and <a href="/wiki/Brian_Jackson_(musician)" title="Brian Jackson (musician)">Brian Jackson</a> song <i>Angel Dust</i>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>In June 2017, Nas appeared in the award-winning 2017 documentary <i><a href="/wiki/The_American_Epic_Sessions" title="The American Epic Sessions">The American Epic Sessions</a></i> directed by <a href="/wiki/Bernard_MacMahon_(filmmaker)" title="Bernard MacMahon (filmmaker)">Bernard MacMahon</a>, where he recorded live <a href="/wiki/Direct-to-disc_recording" title="Direct-to-disc recording">direct-to-disc</a> on the restored first <a href="/wiki/History_of_sound_recording#The_electrical_era_(1925_to_1945)_(including_sound_on_film)" title="History of sound recording">electrical sound recording</a> system from the 1920s.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> He performed "On the Road Again", a 1928 song by the <a href="/wiki/Memphis_Jug_Band" title="Memphis Jug Band">Memphis Jug Band</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> which received universal acclaim with <i><a href="/wiki/The_Hollywood_Reporter" title="The Hollywood Reporter">The Hollywood Reporter</a></i> describing his performance as "fantastic"<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> and the <i><a href="/wiki/Financial_Times" title="Financial Times">Financial Times</a></i> praising his "superb cover of the Memphis Jug Band's "On the Road Again", exposing the hip-hop blueprint within the 1928 stomper."<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> "On the Road Again", and a performance of "One Mic",<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> were released on <i><a href="/wiki/Music_from_The_American_Epic_Sessions:_Original_Motion_Picture_Soundtrack" title="Music from The American Epic Sessions: Original Motion Picture Soundtrack">Music from The American Epic Sessions: Original Motion Picture Soundtrack</a></i> on June 9, 2017.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In April 2018, <a href="/wiki/Kanye_West" title="Kanye West">Kanye West</a> announced on <a href="/wiki/Twitter" title="Twitter">Twitter</a> that Nas's twelfth studio album will be released on June 15, also serving as executive producer for the album.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> The album was announced the day before release, titled <i><a href="/wiki/Nasir_(album)" title="Nasir (album)">Nasir</a></i>.
</p><p>Following the release of <i>Nasir</i>, Nas confirmed he would return to completing a previous album, including production from <a href="/wiki/Swizz_Beatz" title="Swizz Beatz">Swizz Beatz</a> and <a href="/wiki/RZA" title="RZA">RZA</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> This project was released as <i><a href="/wiki/The_Lost_Tapes_2" title="The Lost Tapes 2">The Lost Tapes 2</a></i> on July 19, 2019, which included production from Kanye West, Pharrell Williams, Swizz Beatz, The Alchemist, and RZA. This album was a sequel to Nas's 2002 release, <i>The Lost Tapes</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="2020.E2.80.93present:_King.27s_Disease_series_and_Magic_series"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="2020–present:_King's_Disease_series_and_Magic_series">2020–present: <i>King's Disease</i> series and <i>Magic</i> series</span></h3>
<p>In August 2020, Nas announced that he would be releasing his 13th album. On August 13, he revealed the album's title, <i><a href="/wiki/King%27s_Disease" title="King&#39;s Disease">King's Disease</a></i>. The album, executive-produced by <a href="/wiki/Hit-Boy" title="Hit-Boy">Hit-Boy</a>, was preceded by the lead single, "<a href="/wiki/Ultra_Black" title="Ultra Black">Ultra Black</a>", a song detailing perseverance and pride "despite the system".<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> The album won the <a href="/wiki/Grammy_Award_for_Best_Rap_Album" title="Grammy Award for Best Rap Album">Grammy Award for Best Rap Album</a> at the <a href="/wiki/63rd_Annual_Grammy_Awards" title="63rd Annual Grammy Awards">63rd Annual Grammy Awards</a>, becoming Nas' first Grammy.<sup id="cite_ref-Revolt_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Revolt-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> The sequel album, <i><a href="/wiki/King%27s_Disease_II" title="King&#39;s Disease II">King's Disease II</a></i>, was released on August 6, 2021,<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> and included the song "<a href="/wiki/Nobody_(Nas_and_Lauryn_Hill_song)" title="Nobody (Nas and Lauryn Hill song)">Nobody</a>" featuring <a href="/wiki/Lauryn_Hill" title="Lauryn Hill">Lauryn Hill</a>. <i>King's Disease II</i> debuted at number-three on the US <a href="/wiki/Billboard_200" title="Billboard 200"><i>Billboard</i> 200</a>, becoming Nas's highest-charting album since 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> On December 24, Nas released the album <i><a href="/wiki/Magic_(Nas_album)" title="Magic (Nas album)">Magic</a></i>. It is his third album executive produced by Hit-Boy, and includes guest appearances from <a href="/wiki/ASAP_Rocky" title="ASAP Rocky">ASAP Rocky</a> and <a href="/wiki/DJ_Premier" title="DJ Premier">DJ Premier</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Nas's third installment in the <i>King's Disease</i> series, <i><a href="/wiki/King%27s_Disease_III" title="King&#39;s Disease III">King's Disease III</a></i>, was released the following year. Like its two predecessors, <i>King's Disease III</i> was mainly produced by Hit-Boy; however, it was notably Nas's first studio album to forgo any guest appearances from outside artists.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> Upon release, <i>King's Disease III</i> would become one of the most critically acclaimed albums of Nas's career, becoming his highest-scoring new studio album on review aggregator <a href="/wiki/Metacritic" title="Metacritic">Metacritic</a> and receiving critical praise for the cohesion of Hit-Boy's production with Nas's storytelling and lyricism.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:0_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> Praising <i>King's Disease III,</i> British music publication <a href="/wiki/NME" title="NME">NME</a> stated that Nas, "three decades in, [is] still a force to be reckoned with", while Marcus Shorter of <a href="/wiki/Consequence_(publication)" title="Consequence (publication)">Consequence</a> would write that the album was Nas's and Hit-Boy's "most focused and confident collaboration" and that Nas was "at peace with his legacy, life, and the fact that old age is inevitable".<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:0_109-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On September 12, 2023, Nas announced the 3rd installment to the <i>Magic</i> album series, <i>Magic 3</i>, which would be expected to release two days later, on his 50th birthday.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup>  The album would be the 6th and final collaboration between Nas and <a href="/wiki/Hit-Boy" title="Hit-Boy">Hit-Boy</a> on an album.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Artistry">Artistry</span></h2>
<p>Nas has been praised for his ability to create a "devastating match between lyrics and production" by journalist <a href="/wiki/Peter_Shapiro_(journalist)" title="Peter Shapiro (journalist)">Peter Shapiro</a>, as well as creating a "potent evocation of life on the street", and he has even been compared to <a href="/wiki/Rakim" title="Rakim">Rakim</a> for his lyrical technique. In his book <i><a href="/wiki/Book_of_Rhymes" title="Book of Rhymes">Book of Rhymes: The Poetics of Hip Hop</a></i> (2009), writer Adam Bradley states, "Nas is perhaps contemporary rap's greatest innovator in storytelling. His catalog includes songs narrated before birth ('Fetus') and after death ('Amongst Kings'), biographies ('UBR [Unauthorized Biography of Rakim]') and autobiographies ('Doo Rags'), allegorical tales ('Money Is My Bitch') and epistolary ones ('One Love'), he's rapped in the voice of a woman ('Sekou Story') and even of a gun ('I Gave You Power')."<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Robert_Christgau" title="Robert Christgau">Robert Christgau</a> writes that "Nas has been transfiguring [<a href="/wiki/Gangsta_rap" title="Gangsta rap">gangsta rap</a>] since <i>Illmatic</i>".<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Kool_Moe_Dee" title="Kool Moe Dee">Kool Moe Dee</a> notes that Nas has an "off-beat conversational flow" in his book <i><a href="/wiki/There%27s_a_God_on_the_Mic" title="There&#39;s a God on the Mic">There's a God on the Mic</a></i> – he says: "before Nas, every <a href="/wiki/MC" class="mw-redirect" title="MC">MC</a> focused on rhyming with a cadence that ultimately put the words that rhymed on beat with the <a href="/wiki/Snare_drum" title="Snare drum">snare drum</a>. Nas created a style of <a href="/wiki/Rapping" title="Rapping">rapping</a> that was more conversational than ever before".<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/O.C._(rapper)" title="O.C. (rapper)">OC</a> of <a href="/wiki/D.I.T.C." title="D.I.T.C.">D.I.T.C.</a> comments in the book <i><a href="/wiki/How_to_Rap" title="How to Rap">How to Rap</a></i>: "Nas did the song backwards ['Rewind']... that was a brilliant idea".<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> Also in <i><a href="/wiki/How_to_Rap" title="How to Rap">How to Rap</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/2Mex" title="2Mex">2Mex</a> of <a href="/wiki/The_Visionaries" class="mw-redirect" title="The Visionaries">The Visionaries</a> describes Nas's flow as "effervescent",<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Rah_Digga" title="Rah Digga">Rah Digga</a> says Nas's lyrics have "intricacy",<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bootie_Brown" class="mw-redirect" title="Bootie Brown">Bootie Brown</a> of <a href="/wiki/The_Pharcyde" title="The Pharcyde">The Pharcyde</a> explains that Nas does not always have to make words rhyme as he is "charismatic",<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> and Nas is also described as having a "densely packed"<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> flow, with <a href="/wiki/Compound_rhymes" class="mw-redirect" title="Compound rhymes">compound rhymes</a> that "run over from one beat into the next or even into another bar".<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>About.com ranked him 1st on their list of the "50 Greatest MCs of All Time" in 2014, and a year later, Nas was featured on the "10 Best Rappers of All Time" list by Billboard. <i><a href="/wiki/The_Source_(magazine)" class="mw-redirect" title="The Source (magazine)">The Source</a></i> ranked him No. 2 on their list of the <i>Top 50 Lyricists of All Time</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> In 2013, Nas was ranked fourth on MTV's "Hottest MCs in the Game" list.<sup id="cite_ref-03/12/2013_10:30:00_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-03/12/2013_10:30:00-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> His debut, <i>Illmatic</i>, is widely considered among the greatest hip hop albums of all time.<sup id="cite_ref-Acclaimed_-MTV_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Acclaimed_-MTV-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Controversies_and_feuds">Controversies and feuds</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Jay-Z">Jay-Z</span></h3>
<p>Initially friends, Nas and Jay-Z had met a number of times in the 1990s with no animosity between the two. Jay-Z requested that Nas appear on his 1996 album <i><a href="/wiki/Reasonable_Doubt_(album)" title="Reasonable Doubt (album)">Reasonable Doubt</a></i> on the track "Bring it On"; however, Nas never showed up to the studio and was not included on the album. In response to this, Jay-Z asked producer Ski Beatz to sample a line from Nas's song <i><a href="/wiki/The_World_Is_Yours_(Nas_song)" title="The World Is Yours (Nas song)">The World is Yours</a></i>, with the sample featured heavily in what went on to be <i><a href="/wiki/Dead_Presidents_II" class="mw-redirect" title="Dead Presidents II">Dead Presidents II</a></i>. The two traded subliminal responses for the next couple of years, until the beef was escalated further in 2001 after Jay-Z publicly addressed Nas at the <a href="/wiki/Summer_Jam_(festival)" title="Summer Jam (festival)">Summer Jam</a>, performing what would go on to be known as "<a href="/wiki/Takeover_(Jay-Z_song)" title="Takeover (Jay-Z song)">Takeover</a>", ending the performance by saying "ask Nas, he don't want it with Hov". After Jay-Z eventually released the song on his 2001 album <a href="/wiki/The_Blueprint_(album)" class="mw-redirect" title="The Blueprint (album)">The Blueprint</a>, Nas responded with the song "<a href="/wiki/Ether_(song)" title="Ether (song)">Ether</a>", from his album <i><a href="/wiki/Stillmatic" title="Stillmatic">Stillmatic</a></i>, with both fans and critics saying that the song had effectively saved Nas's career and marked his return to prominence, and almost unanimously agreeing Nas had won their feud. Jay-Z responded with a freestyle over the instrumental to Nas's "<a href="/wiki/Got_Ur_Self_a_Gun" title="Got Ur Self a Gun">Got Ur Self a Gun</a>", known as "Supa Ugly". In the song, Jay-Z makes reference to Nas's girlfriend and daughter, going into graphic detail about having an affair with his girlfriend.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-npr_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-npr-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> Jay-Z's mother was personally disgusted by the song, and demanded he apologise to Nas and his family, which he did in December 2001 on <a href="/wiki/Hot_97" class="mw-redirect" title="Hot 97">Hot 97</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> Supa Ugly marked the last direct diss song between Jay-Z and Nas, however, the two continued to trade subliminals on their subsequent releases. The feud was officially brought to an end in 2005, when Jay-Z and Nas performed on stage together in a surprise concert also featuring <a href="/wiki/P_Diddy" class="mw-redirect" title="P Diddy">P Diddy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kanye_West" title="Kanye West">Kanye West</a> and <a href="/wiki/Beanie_Sigel" title="Beanie Sigel">Beanie Sigel</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> The following year, Nas signed with <a href="/wiki/Def_Jam_Recordings" title="Def Jam Recordings">Def Jam Recordings</a>, of which Jay-Z then served as president.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Cam.27ron"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Cam'ron">Cam'ron</span></h3>
